href="/wiki/Template:Punjabis" title="Template:Punjabis"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Punjabis" title="Template talk:Punjabis"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Punjabis&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Punjabi</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/\xca\x8c/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;cut&#39;">\xca\x8c</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/d\xca\x92/: &#39;j&#39; in &#39;jam&#39;">d\xca\x92</span><span title="/\xc9\x91\xcb\x90/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">\xc9\x91\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhi</a>: <span lang="pa" title="Punjabi language text">\xe0\xa8\xaa\xe0\xa9\xb0\xe0\xa8\x9c\xe0\xa8\xbe\xe0\xa8\xac\xe0\xa9\x80</span> <i lang="pa-Latn" title="Punjabi-language transliteration"><i>p\xc3\xa3j\xc4\x81b\xc4\xab</i></i>; <a href="/wiki/Shahmukhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Shahmukhi">Shahmukhi</a>: <span lang="pnb" title="Punjabi language text"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, IranNastaliq, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 125%; font-style: normal;">\xd9\xbe\xd9\x86\xd8\xac\xd8\xa7\xd8\xa8\xdb\x8c</span>&#x202c;</span> <i lang="pa-Latn" title="American Library Association \xe2\x80\x93 Library of Congress transliteration">pa\xe1\xb9\x89j\xc4\x81b\xc4\xab</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-KachruKachru2008_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KachruKachru2008-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> is an <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Aryan language">Indo-Aryan language</a> with more than 100&#160;million native speakers worldwide. It is the native language of the <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjabi people">Punjabi people</a>, an ethnic group of the cultural region of <a href="/wiki/Punjab_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a>. The Punjab extends from northwest <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> through eastern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.\n</p><p>As of 2015, Punjabi is the 10th <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">most widely spoken language</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-NE100_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NE100-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NE2_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NE2-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It is <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Pakistan#Punjabi" title="Languages of Pakistan">the most widely spoken language in Pakistan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Pakistan_Census_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pakistan_Census-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> the 11th most widely <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers_in_India" title="List of languages by number of native speakers in India">spoken language in India</a>, and the third most-spoken native language in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Subcontinent" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Subcontinent">Indian Subcontinent</a>. In Canada, it is the fifth <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Canada#Speakers_by_mother_tongue" title="Languages of Canada">most-spoken native language</a>, after <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandarin language">Mandarin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cantonese language">Cantonese</a>. It has a significant presence in the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>.\n</p><p>Punjabi is unusual among <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a> in its use of <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">lexical tone</a> (that is, the way in which the pitch of the voice conveys meaning).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The Punjabi language is written in one of two alphabets: <a href="/wiki/Shahmukhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Shahmukhi">Shahmukhi</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhi</a>. In the Punjab, both writing systems are used (a rare occurrence called <a href="/wiki/Synchronic_digraphia" class="mw-redirect" title="Synchronic digraphia">synchronic digraphia</a>): Shahmukhi is used mainly by <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_Muslims" title="Punjabi Muslims">Punjabi Muslims</a>, and Gurmukhi is used mainly by <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_Sikhs" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjabi Sikhs">Punjabi Sikhs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_Hindus" title="Punjabi Hindus">Hindus</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Punjabi_language" title="History of the Punjabi language">History of the Punjabi language</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>\n<p>The word Punjabi has been derived from the word Panj-\xc4\x81b, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> for "Five Waters", referring to the five major eastern tributaries of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a>.  The name of the region was introduced by the <a href="/wiki/Turko-Persian" class="mw-redirect" title="Turko-Persian">Turko-Persian</a> conquerors<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> and was a translation of the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> name for the region, <i>Panchanada</i>, which means "Land of the Five Rivers".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <i>Panj</i> is <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i>\xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa4\x9e\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\x9a (<i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration">pa\xc3\xb1ca</i>)</i> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i><i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration">\xcf\x80\xce\xad\xce\xbd\xcf\x84\xce\xb5 (p\xc3\xa9nte)</i></i> "five", and "\xc4\x81b" is cognate with Sanskrit <i>\xe0\xa4\x85\xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa5\x8d (\xc3\xa1p)</i> and with the <i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration">Av-</i> of <i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration">Avon</i>. The historical <a href="/wiki/Punjab_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjab region">Punjab region</a>, now divided between India and Pakistan, is defined physiographically by the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a> and these five tributaries. One of the five, the <a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">Beas River</a>, is a tributary of another, the <a href="/wiki/Sutlej" title="Sutlej">Sutlej</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tilla_Jogian.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Tilla_Jogian.jpg/220px-Tilla_Jogian.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Tilla_Jogian.jpg/330px-Tilla_Jogian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Tilla_Jogian.jpg/440px-Tilla_Jogian.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tilla_Jogian.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tilla_Jogian" title="Tilla Jogian">Tilla Jogian</a>,district Jehlum,Punjab,Pakistan a hilltop associated with many Nath jogis(considered among compilers  of earlier Punjabi works)</div></div></div>\n<p>Punjabi developed from Sanskrit through Prakrit languages and later Apabhra\xe1\xb9\x83\xc5\x9ba (Sanskrit: \xe0\xa4\x85\xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa4\xad\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa4\x82\xe0\xa4\xb6; corruption or corrupted speech)<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> From 600&#160;BC Sanskrit gave birth to many regional languages in different parts of India. All these languages are called <a href="/wiki/Prakrit_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Prakrit language">Prakrit</a> (Sanskrit: \xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\x95\xe0\xa5\x83\xe0\xa4\xa4 pr\xc4\x81k\xe1\xb9\x9bta) collectively. <a href="/wiki/Shauraseni_language" title="Shauraseni language">Shauraseni Prakrit</a> was one of these Prakrit languages, which was spoken in north and north-western India and Punjabi and western dialects of Hindi developed from this Prakrit. Later in northern India <a href="/wiki/Shauraseni_language" title="Shauraseni language">Shauraseni Prakrit</a> gave rise to Shauraseni Aparbhsha, a descendent of Prakrit. Punjabi emerged as an Apabhramsha, a degenerated form of Prakrit, in the 7th century A.D. and became stable by the 10th century. By the 10th century, many <a href="/wiki/Nath" title="Nath">Nath</a> poets were associated with earlier Punjabi works.<sup id="cite_ref-India_1952._Page_148_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-India_1952._Page_148-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baldev_Raj_Nayar_1969._Page_35_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baldev_Raj_Nayar_1969._Page_35-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baldev_Raj_Nayar_1969._Page_35_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baldev_Raj_Nayar_1969._Page_35-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Sauraseni_Pr\xc4\x81krit_Language_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Sauraseni_Pr\xc4\x81krit_Language-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Sauraseni_Pr\xc4\x81krit_Language_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Sauraseni_Pr\xc4\x81krit_Language-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arabic_and_Persian_influence_on_Punjabi">Arabic and Persian influence on Punjabi</span></h3>\n<p>Arabic and Persian influence in the historical Punjab region began with the late first millennium <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests_on_the_Indian_subcontinent" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquests on the Indian subcontinent">Muslim conquests on the Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-google_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The Persian language was introduced in the subcontinent a few centuries later by various Persianized Central Asian <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Afghanistan" title="Demographics of Afghanistan">Afghan</a> dynasties including that of <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_of_Ghazni" title="Mahmud of Ghazni">Mahmud of Ghazni</a>. Many Persian and Arabic words were incorporated in Punjabi.<sup id="cite_ref-google2_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Punjabi has more Persian and Arabic vocabulary than <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marathi_language" title="Marathi language">Marathi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_language" title="Gujarati language">Gujarati</a> due to the proximity of the Punjab with <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">western Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-google3_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google3-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> It is noteworthy that the <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani language</a> divided into Hindi, with more Sanskritisation, and Urdu, with more Persianisation, but in Punjabi both Sanskrit and Persian words are used with a liberal approach to language. Later, it was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, though these influences have been minor in comparison to Persian and Arabic. However, in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, English words in the official language are more widespread than <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-google4_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google4-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span></h2>\n<p>Punjabi is the most widely spoken language in Pakistan, the eleventh -most widely spoken in India and spoken Punjabi diaspora in various countries.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pakistan_ethnic_map_1973-v2.svg" class="image"><img alt="" script, which is used in Pakistan.</div></div></div>\n<p>Punjabi is the most widely spoken language in Pakistan, being the native language of 44% of its population. It is the provincial language in the <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">Punjab Province</a>.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<caption><b>Census history of Punjabi speakers the United States, Australia, the United Kingdom, and Canada, where it is the fourth-most-commonly used language.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\nThere were 76&#160;million Punjabi speakers in Pakistan in 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> 33&#160;million in India in 2011,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> 368,000 in Canada in 2006,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and smaller numbers in other countries.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Countries_where_Punjabi_is_spoken.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Countries_where_Punjabi_is_spoken.png/220px-Countries_where_Punjabi_is_spoken.png" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;100,000 - 200,000</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;50,000 - 100,000</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1,000 - 50,000</div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span></h2>\n<p>Despite Punjabi\'s rich literary history, it was not until 1947 that it would be recognized as an official language. Previous governments in the area of the Punjab had favoured Persian, Hindustani, or even earlier standardised versions of local registers as the language of the court or government. After the annexation of the <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Empire" title="Sikh Empire">Sikh Empire</a> by the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">British East India Company</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Sikh_War" title="Second Anglo-Sikh War">Second Anglo-Sikh War</a> in 1849, the British policy of establishing a uniform language for administration was expanded into the Punjab. The British Empire employed Hindi and Urdu in its administration of North-Central and North-West India, while in the North-East of India, Bengali was used as the language of administration. Despite its lack of official sanction, the Punjabi language continued to flourish as an instrument of cultural production, with rich literary traditions continuing until modern times. The Sikh religion, with its <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurmukhi alphabet">Gurmukhi</a> script, played a special role in standardising and providing education in the language via <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">Gurdwaras</a>, while writers of all religions continued to produce poetry, prose, and literature in the language.\n</p><p>In India, Punjabi is one of the 22 <a href="/wiki/Scheduled_languages_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Scheduled languages of India">scheduled languages of India</a>. It is the first official language of the <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Indian State of Punjab</a>. Punjabi also has second language official status in <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a> along with Urdu, and in <a href="/wiki/Haryana" title="Haryana">Haryana</a>. \nIn Pakistan, no <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Pakistan" title="Languages of Pakistan">regional ethnic language</a> has been granted official status at the national level, and as such Punjabi is not an official language at the national level, even though it is the most spoken language in Pakistan after Urdu, the national language of Pakistan. It is, however, the official provincial language of <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">Punjab, Pakistan</a>, the second largest and the most populous province of Pakistan as well as in Islamabad Capital Territory. The only two official national languages in Pakistan are <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_English" title="Pakistani English">English</a>, which are considered the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua francas</a> of Pakistan.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Punjabi">Modern Punjabi</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Punjabi_Alphabet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Punjabi_Alphabet.jpg/220px-Punjabi_Alphabet.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Punjabi_Alphabet.jpg/330px-Punjabi_Alphabet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Punjabi_Alphabet.jpg/440px-Punjabi_Alphabet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Punjabi_Alphabet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhi</a> alphabet excluding vowels</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_Punjabi">Standard Punjabi</span></h4>\n<ul><li>Punjabi is spoken in many dialects in an area from <a href="/wiki/Islamabad" title="Islamabad">Islamabad</a> to <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Majhi_dialect" title="Majhi dialect">Majhi dialect</a> has been adopted as standard Punjabi in Pakistan and India for education, media etc. The Majhi (in Shahmukhi \xd9\x85\xd8\xa7\xd8\xac\xda\xbe\xdb\x8c\xd8\x8c in Gurumukhi \xe0\xa8\xae\xe0\xa8\xbe\xe0\xa8\x9d\xe0\xa9\x80) dialect originated in the <a href="/wiki/Majha" title="Majha">Majha</a> region of the Punjab. The Majha region consists central districts of <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_Punjab" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistani Punjab">Pakistani Punjab</a> and in India around <a href="/wiki/Amritsar" title="Amritsar">Amritsar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gurdaspur" title="Gurdaspur">Gurdaspur</a> regions, known. The two most important cities in this area are Lahore and Amritsar.</li>\n<li>In India technical words in Standard Punjabi are loaned from Sanskrit similarly to other major Indian languages, but it generously uses Arabic, Persian, and English words also in the official language. In India, Punjabi is written in the Gurumukh\xc4\xab script in offices, schools, and media. Gurumukhi is considered the standard script for Punjabi, though it is often unofficially written in the Devanagari or Latin scripts due to influence from <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">English</a>, India\'s two primary official languages at the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India" title="Government of India">Union</a>-level.</li>\n<li>In Pakistan, Punjabi is generally written using the Shahmukh\xc4\xab script, created from a modification of the Persian <a href="/wiki/Nasta%CA%BFl%C4%ABq_script" title="Nasta\xca\xbfl\xc4\xabq script">Nasta\xca\xbfl\xc4\xabq script</a>. In Pakistan, Punjabi loans technical words from <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> languages, just like <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> does.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_dialects">Major dialects</span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Majhi_.28Standard_Punjabi.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Majhi_(Standard_Punjabi)">Majhi (Standard Punjabi)</span></h3>\n<p>The Majhi (\xd9\x85\xd8\xa7\xd8\xac\xda\xbe\xdb\x8c \xe0\xa8\xae\xe0\xa8\xbe\xe0\xa8\x9d\xe0\xa9\x80) dialect spoken around <a href="/wiki/Amritsar" title="Amritsar">Amritsar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> is Punjabi\'s <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestige (linguistics)">prestige dialect</a>. Majhi is spoken in the heart of <a href="/wiki/Punjab_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a> in the region of <a href="/wiki/Majha" title="Majha">Majha</a>, which spans <a href="/wiki/Lahore_District" title="Lahore District">Lahore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amritsar_district" title="Amritsar district">Amritsar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gurdaspur_district" title="Gurdaspur district">Gurdaspur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kasur_District" title="Kasur District">Kasur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarn_Taran_district" title="Tarn Taran district">Tarn Taran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faisalabad_District" title="Faisalabad District">Faisalabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nankana_Sahib_District" title="Nankana Sahib District">Nankana Sahib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pathankot_district" title="Pathankot district">Pathankot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Okara_District" title="Okara District">Okara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakpattan_District" title="Pakpattan District">Pakpattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sahiwal_District" title="Sahiwal District">Sahiwal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narowal_District" title="Narowal District">Narowal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheikhupura_District" title="Sheikhupura District">Sheikhupura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sialkot_District" title="Sialkot District">Sialkot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiniot_District" title="Chiniot District">Chiniot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gujranwala_District" title="Gujranwala District">Gujranwala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gujrat_District" title="Gujrat District">Gujrat</a> and in Mandi Bahauddin(97\xe2\x80\x99/.) districts.\n</p><p>Majhi retains the nasal consonants <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/<a href="/wiki/Velar_nasal" title="Velar nasal">\xc5\x8b</a>/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/<a href="/wiki/Palatal_nasal#Palatal_or_alveolo-palatal" title="Palatal nasal">\xc9\xb2</a>/</span>, which have been superseded elsewhere by non-nasals <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_stop" title="Voiced velar stop">\xc9\xa1</a>/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_affricate" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced palato-alveolar affricate">d\xcd\xa1\xca\x92</a>/</span> respectively. The Majhi (and Lahnda) spoken in Pakistan is more <a href="/wiki/Persianization" title="Persianization">Persianized</a> in vocabulary, and the usage of the sounds <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_fricative#Voiced_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced alveolar fricative">z</a>/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">x</a>/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_fricative" title="Voiced velar fricative">\xc9\xa3</a>/</span> is more common. (Tabbar in both dialects is informal in the following table)\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>English\n</th>\n<th>Gurmukhi based (India)\n</th>\n<th>Shahmukhi based (Pakistan)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>President\n</td>\n<td><span lang="pa" title="Punjabi language text">\xe0\xa8\xb0\xe0\xa8\xbe\xe0\xa8\xb8\xe0\xa8\xbc\xe0\xa8\x9f\xe0\xa8\xb0\xe0\xa8\xaa\xe0\xa8\xa4\xe0\xa9\x80</span> (<i lang="pa-Latn" title="Punjabi-language transliteration"><i>r\xc4\x81\xc5\x9btarpat\xc4\xab</i></i>)\n</td>\n<td><span lang="pnb" title="Punjabi language text"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, IranNastaliq, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 125%; font-style: normal;">\xd8\xaf\xd8\xa7\xd8\xb1\xd8\xa7\xd9\x84\xd8\xad\xda\xa9\xd9\x88\xd9\x85\xd8\xaa</span>&#x202c;</span> (<i lang="pa-Latn" title="American Library Association \xe2\x80\x93 Library of Congress transliteration"><i>d\xc4\x81rul hak\xc5\xabmat</i></i>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Viewer\n</td>\n<td><span lang="pa" title="Punjabi language text">\xe0\xa8\xa6\xe0\xa8\xb0\xe0\xa8\xb8\xe0\xa8\xbc\xe0\xa8\x95</span> (<i lang="pa-Latn" title="Punjabi-language transliteration"><i>dar\xc5\x9bak</i></i>)\n</td>\n<td><span lang="pnb" title="Punjabi language text"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, IranNastaliq, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 125%; font-style: normal;">\xd9\x86\xd8\xa7\xd8\xb8\xd8\xb1\xdb\x8c\xd9\x86</span>&#x202c;</span> (<i lang="pa-Latn" title="American Library Association \xe2\x80\x93 Library of Congress transliteration"><i>n\xc4\x81zr\xc4\xabn</i></i>)\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shahpuri">Shahpuri</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Shahpuri_dialect" title="Shahpuri dialect">Shahpuri</a> dialect (also known as Sargodha dialect) is mostly spoken in Pakistani Punjab. Its name is derived from former Shahpur District (now Shahpur Tehsil, being part of Sargodha District). It is spoken throughout a widespread area, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Sargodha" title="Sargodha">Sargodha</a> and Khushab Districts and also spoken in neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Mianwali" title="Mianwali">Mianwali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhakkar" title="Bhakkar">Bhakkar</a> Districts. It is mainly spoken on western end of Sindh River to Chennab river crossing Jehlam river.<sup id="cite_ref-mother_tongue_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mother_tongue-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Malwai">Malwai</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Malwai_dialect" title="Malwai dialect">Malwai</a> is spoken in the eastern part of Indian Punjab and also in <a href="/wiki/Bahawalnagar" title="Bahawalnagar">Bahawalnagar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vehari" title="Vehari">Vehari</a> districts of Pakistan. Main areas are <a href="/wiki/Ludhiana" title="Ludhiana">Ludhiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patiala" title="Patiala">Patiala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ambala" title="Ambala">Ambala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bathinda" title="Bathinda">Bathinda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mansa_district,_Punjab" title="Mansa district, Punjab">Mansa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganganagar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ganganagar">Ganganagar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malerkotla" title="Malerkotla">Malerkotla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fazilka" title="Fazilka">Fazilka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferozepur" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferozepur">Ferozepur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moga_district" title="Moga district">Moga</a>. <a href="/wiki/Malwa" title="Malwa">Malwa</a> is the southern and central part of present-day Indian Punjab. It also includes the Punjabi speaking northern areas of <a href="/wiki/Haryana" title="Haryana">Haryana</a>, viz. <a href="/wiki/Ambala" title="Ambala">Ambala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hisar,_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Hisar, India">Hissar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sirsa,_Haryana" class="mw-redirect" title="Sirsa, Haryana">Sirsa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurukshetra" title="Kurukshetra">Kurukshetra</a> etc. Not to be confused with the Malvi language, which shares its name.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Doabi">Doabi</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Doabi" class="mw-redirect" title="Doabi">Doabi</a> is spoken in both the Indian Punjab as well as parts of Pakistan Punjab owing to post-1947 migration of Muslim populace from East Punjab. The word "Do Aabi" means "the land between two rivers" and this dialect was historically spoken between the rivers of <a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">the Beas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sutlej_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Sutlej River">the Sutlej</a> in the region called <a href="/wiki/Doaba" title="Doaba">Doaba</a>. Regions it is presently spoken includes the <a href="/wiki/Jalandhar_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Jalandhar District">Jalandhar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hoshiarpur_district" title="Hoshiarpur district">Hoshiarpur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kapurthala_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Kapurthala District">Kapurthala</a> districts in Indian Punjab, specifically in the areas known as the Dona and <a href="/wiki/Manjki_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="Manjki Region">Manjki</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Toba_Tek_Singh_District" title="Toba Tek Singh District">Toba Tek Singh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Faisalabad_District" title="Faisalabad District">Faisalabad</a> districts in Pakistan Punjab where the dialect is known as Faisalabadi Punjabi.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pwadhi">Pwadhi</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pwadhi_dialect" title="Pwadhi dialect">Pwadhi</a>, Powadh, Puadh or Powadha is a region of Punjab and parts of Haryana between the Satluj and Ghaggar rivers. The part lying south, south-east and east of Rupnagar adjacent to <a href="/wiki/Ambala" title="Ambala">Ambala</a> District (Haryana) is Powadhi. The Powadh extends from that part of the <a href="/wiki/Rupnagar" title="Rupnagar">Rupnagar</a> District which lies near Satluj to beyond the Ghaggar river in the east up to Kala Amb, which is at the border of the states of Himachal pradesh and Haryana. Parts of <a href="/wiki/Fatehgarh_Sahib" title="Fatehgarh Sahib">Fatehgarh Sahib</a> district, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Patiala" title="Patiala">Patiala</a> districts like Rajpura are also part of Powadh. The language is spoken over a large area in present Punjab as well as Haryana. In Punjab, Kharar, Kurali, Ropar, Nurpurbedi, Morinda, Pail, Rajpura and Samrala are the areas where the Puadhi is spoken and the dialect area also includes Pinjore, Kalka, Ismailabad, Pehowa to Bangar area in <a href="/wiki/Fatehabad_district" title="Fatehabad district">Fatehabad district</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Jhangochi.2FChangvi"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jhangochi/Changvi">Jhangochi/Changvi</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Jhangochi_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Jhangochi dialect">Jhangochi</a> (\xd8\xac\xda\xbe\xd9\x86\xda\xaf\xd9\x88\xda\x86\xdb\x8c) dialect is spoken in Pakistani Punjab throughout a widespread area, starting from <a href="/wiki/Khanewal" title="Khanewal">Khanewal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jhang" title="Jhang">Jhang</a> at both ends of Ravi and Chenab to Hafizabad district.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Jangli.2FRachnavi"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jangli/Rachnavi">Jangli/Rachnavi</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Jangli_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Jangli dialect">Jangli</a> is a dialect of former nomad tribes of areas whose names are often suffixed with \'Bar\' derived from jungle bar before irrigation system arrived in the start of the 20th century, for example, Sandal Bar, Kirana Bar, Neeli Bar, Ganji Bar. Former Layllpur and western half of Montgomary district used to speak this dialect.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chenavari">Chenavari</span></h3>\n<p>West of Chenaab river in Jhang district of Pakistani Punjab the dialect of Jhangochi merges with Thalochi and resultant dialect is Chenavari. Name is derived from Chenaab river.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center;">\n<caption><b><a href="/wiki/Vowel" the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Retroflex_approximant" title="Retroflex approximant">\xc9\xbb</a></span> \xe0\xa8\xb2\xe0\xa8\xbc<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant#Palatal" title="Palatal approximant">j</a></span> \xe0\xa8\xaf\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_glottal_fricative" title="Voiced glottal fricative">\xc9\xa6</a></span> \xe0\xa8\xb9\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tone">Tone</span></h3>\n<p>Punjabi is a tonal language and in any word there is a choice of three tones, high-falling, low-rising, and level (neutral):<sup id="cite_ref-Bailey_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bowden_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowden-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>k\xc3\xa0r</i> (high-falling) "house"</li>\n<li><i>k\xc3\xa1r</i> (low-rising) "dandruff"</li>\n<li><i>kar</i> (level) "do/hands"</li>\n<li><i>k\xc3\xb2\xe1\xb9\x9b\xc4\x81</i> (high-falling) "horse"</li>\n<li><i>k\xc3\xb3\xe1\xb9\x9b\xc4\x81</i> (low-rising) "leper"</li>\n<li><i>ko\xe1\xb9\x9b\xc4\x81</i> (level) "whip"</li></ul>\n<p>Level tone is found in about 75% of words and is described by some as absence of tone.<sup id="cite_ref-Bailey_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> There are also some words which are said to have rising tone in the first syllable and falling in the second. (Some writers describe this as a fourth tone.)<sup id="cite_ref-Bailey_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> However, a recent acoustic study of six Punjabi speakers in America found no evidence of a separate falling tone following a medial consonant.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>m\xc3\xb3\xe1\xb8\x8d\xc3\xa0</i> (rising-falling) "shoulder"</li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="9.665306122449" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Punjabi_specific_sounds.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/93/Punjabi_specific_sounds.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (118 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="118006" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/9/93/Punjabi_specific_sounds.ogg/Punjabi_specific_sounds.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="197120" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Punjabi_specific_sounds.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some Punjabi distinct tones for gh, jh, \xe1\xb8\x8dh, dh, bh</div></div></div>\n<p>It is considered that these tones arose when voiced aspirated consonants (gh, jh, \xe1\xb8\x8dh, dh, bh) lost their aspiration. At the beginning of a word they became voiceless unaspirated consonants (k, c, \xe1\xb9\xad, t, p) followed by a high-falling tone; medially or finally they became voiced unaspirated consonants (g, j, \xe1\xb8\x8d, d, b), preceded by a low-rising tone. (The development of a high-falling tone apparently did not take place in every word, but only in those which historically had a long vowel.)<sup id="cite_ref-Bowden_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowden-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The presence of an [h] (although the [h] is now silent or very weakly pronounced except word-initially) word-finally (and sometimes medially) also often causes a rising tone before it, for example <i>c\xc3\xa1(h)</i> "tea".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurmukhi script">Gurmukhi script</a> which was developed in the 16th century has separate letters for voiced aspirated sounds, so it is thought that the change in pronunciation of the consonants and development of tones may have taken place since that time.<sup id="cite_ref-Bowden_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowden-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some other languages in Pakistan have also been found to have tonal distinctions, including <a href="/wiki/Burushaski" title="Burushaski">Burushaski</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gujari_language" title="Gujari language">Gujari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindko" title="Hindko">Hindko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalami_language" title="Kalami language">Kalami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shina_language" title="Shina language">Shina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Torwali_language" title="Torwali language">Torwali</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_grammar" title="Punjabi grammar">Punjabi grammar</a></div>\n<p>The grammar of the Punjabi language concerns the word order, case marking, verb conjugation, and other morphological and syntactic structures of the Punjabi language. The main article discusses the grammar of Modern Standard Punjabi as defined by the sources cited therein.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_systems">Writing systems</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Shahmukh%C4%AB_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Shahmukh\xc4\xab alphabet">Shahmukh\xc4\xab alphabet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gurmukh%C4%AB_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurmukh\xc4\xab alphabet">Gurmukh\xc4\xab alphabet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_braille" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjabi braille">Punjabi braille</a></div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Shahmukhi_alphabet" title="Shahmukhi alphabet">Shahmukhi alphabet</a></th></tr><tr><td class="hlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<p><span lang="pnb" title="Punjabi language text"><span style="font-size: 125%; line-height: 170%;">\n<span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, IranNastaliq, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; />Extended <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Perso-Arabic script</a></th></tr><tr><td class="hlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Arabic_alphabet" title="History of the Arabic alphabet">History</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Transliteration_of_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Transliteration of Arabic">Transliteration</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arabic_diacritics" title="Arabic diacritics">Diacritics</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hamza" title="Hamza">Hamza</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Arabic_numerals" title="Eastern Arabic numerals">Numerals</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Abjad_numerals" title="Abjad numerals">Numeration</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Arabic-script_sidebar" title="Template:Arabic-script sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Arabic-script_sidebar" title="Template talk:Arabic-script sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Arabic-script_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Punjabi has two major writing systems in use: Gurmukhi, which is a <a href="/wiki/Brahmic_scripts" title="Brahmic scripts">Brahmic script</a> derived from the <a href="/wiki/La%E1%B9%87%E1%B8%8D%C4%81_scripts" title="La\xe1\xb9\x87\xe1\xb8\x8d\xc4\x81 scripts">La\xe1\xb9\x87\xe1\xb8\x8d\xc4\x81 script</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and Shahmukhi, which is an <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a>. The word Gurmukhi means "from the Guru\'s mouth",<sup id="cite_ref-Khalsa_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khalsa-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and Shahmukhi means "from the King\'s mouth".<sup id="cite_ref-Saini,_Tejinder_2008_p._177_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saini,_Tejinder_2008_p._177-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Punjab province of Pakistan, the script used is Shahmukhi and differs from the <a href="/wiki/Urdu_alphabet" title="Urdu alphabet">Urdu alphabet</a> in having four additional letters.<sup id="cite_ref-UCLA-Orthography_of_Punjabi_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCLA-Orthography_of_Punjabi-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/States_and_union_territories_of_India" title="States and union territories of India">Indian states</a> of <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haryana" title="Haryana">Haryana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a> and other parts of India, the Gurmukh\xc4\xab script is generally used for writing Punjabi.<sup id="cite_ref-UCLA-Orthography_of_Punjabi_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCLA-Orthography_of_Punjabi-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Historically, various local Brahmic scripts including La\xe1\xb9\x87\xe1\xb8\x8d\xc4\x81 were also in use.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_text">Sample text</span></h2>\n<p>This sample text was taken from the Punjabi Wikipedia article on <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>.\n</p>\n<p><b><a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhi</a>:</b> va\xe1\xb8\x8d\xe1\xb8\x8d\xc4\x81 \xc5\xa1\xc3\xa1ir \xc3\xa0i. lahor p\xc4\x81kist\xc4\x81n d\xc4\x81 si\xc4\x81s\xc4\xab, r\xc3\xa1tal\xc4\xab te pa\xe1\xb9\x9b\xc4\x81\xcc\x80\xc4\xab d\xc4\x81 g\xc3\xa1\xe1\xb9\x9b \xc3\xa0i te is la\xc4\xab \xc3\xadn\xc5\xab\xcc\x83 p\xc4\x81kist\xc4\x81n d\xc4\x81 dil v\xc4\xab kih\xc4\x81 j\xc4\x81nd\xc4\x81 \xc3\xa0i. lahaur dari\xc4\x81e r\xc4\x81v\xc4\xab de ka\xe1\xb9\x87\xe1\xb8\x8d\xc3\xa8 te vasd\xc4\x81 \xc3\xa0i. te isd\xc4\xab lok gi\xe1\xb9\x87t\xc4\xab ikk karo\xe1\xb9\x9b de ne\xe1\xb9\x9be \xc3\xa0i.\n</p><p><b><a href="/wiki/Translation" title="Translation">Translation</a></b>: Lahore is the capital city of the Pakistani Punjab. After a number of people from <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a>, Lahore is the second largest city. Lahore is Pakistan\'s political stronghold and education capital and so it is also the heart of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>. Lahore lies on the bank of the <a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi River</a>. And, its population is close to ten million people.\n</p><p><b><a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a>:</b> <span title="Representation in the \xc9\xbe\xc9\x99\xcc\x81\xc9\xa6t\xcc\xaa\xc9\x99\xcc\x84l\xc4\xab\xcb\x90 t\xcc\xaa\xc4\x93 p\xc9\x99\xcc\x84\xc9\xbd\xc9\xa6\xc3\xa0\xcb\x90\xc4\xab\xcb\x90 d\xcc\xaa\xc4\x81\xcb\x90 \xc9\xa1\xc9\x99\xcc\x81\xc9\xbd\xc9\xa6 \xc9\xa6\xc9\x9b\xcc\x80\xcb\x90 t\xcc\xaa\xc4\x93 \xc9\xaa\xcc\x84s l\xc9\x99\xcc\x84\xc4\xab\xcb\x90 \xc9\xaa\xcc\x81\xc9\xa6n\xc5\xab\xcc\x83 d\xcd\xa1\xca\x92\xc4\x81\xcc\x83\xcb\x90d\xcc\xaa\xc4\x81\xcb\x90 \xc9\xa6\xc9\x9b\xcc\x80\xcb\x90 \xe2\x80\x96 l\xc9\x99\xcc\x84\xc9\xa6\xc9\x94\xcc\x84\xcb\x90\xc9\xbe d\xcc\xaa\xc9\x99\xcc\x84\xc9\xbe\xc9\xaa\xcc\x84\xc4\x81\xcb\x90\xc4\x93 \xc9\xbe\xc4\x81\xcb\x90\xca\x8b\xc4\xab\xcb\x90 d\xcc\xaa\xc4\x93 k\xc9\x99\xcc\x84\xcc\x83\xca\x88\xc3\xa8 t\xcc\xaa\xc4\x93 \xca\x8b\xc9\x99\xcc\x84\xcc\x83sd\xcc\xaa\xc4\xab\xcb\x90 \xc9\xa6\xc9\x9b\xcc\x80\xcb\x90 \xe2\x80\x96 t\xcc\xaa\xc4\x93 \xc4\xabsd\xcc\xaa\xc4\xab\xcb\x90 l\xc5\x8dk \xc9\xa1\xc9\xaa\xcc\x84\xc9\xb3t\xcc\xaa\xc4\xab\xcb\x90 \xc9\xaa\xcc\x84k\xcb\x90 k\xc9\x99\xcc\x84\xc9\xbe\xc5\x8d\xc9\xbd d\xcc\xaa\xc4\x93 n\xc4\x93\xc9\xbd\xc4\x93 \xc9\xa6\xc9\x9b\xcc\x80\xcb\x90 \xe2\x80\x96]</span>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_development">Literature development</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_literature" title="Punjabi literature">Punjabi literature</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Medieval_era.2C_Mughal_and_Sikh_period"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_era,_Mughal_and_Sikh_period">Medieval era, Mughal and Sikh period</span></h3>\n<ul><li>The earliest Punjabi literature is found in the fragments of writings of the 11th Nath yogis (\xe0\xa8\xa8\xe0\xa8\xbe\xe0\xa8\xa5\xe0\xa8\xaf\xe0\xa9\x8b\xe0\xa8\x97\xe0\xa9\x80, <span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, IranNastaliq, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 125%; font-style: normal;">\xd9\x86\xd8\xa7\xd8\xaa\xda\xbe\xdb\x8c\xd9\x88\xda\xaf\xdb\x8c</span>&#x202c;) Gorakshanath and Charpatnah which is primarily spiritual and mystical in tone. <a href="/wiki/Fariduddin_Ganjshakar" title="Fariduddin Ganjshakar">Fariduddin Ganjshakar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pak_Pattan" class="mw-redirect" title="Pak Pattan">Pak Pattan</a> is generally recognised as the first major poet of the Punjabi language.<sup id="cite_ref-sikh_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sikh-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Roughly from the 11th century to 19th century, many great Sufi saints and poets preached in the Punjabi language. <a href="/wiki/Bulle_Shah" class="mw-redirect" title="Bulle Shah">Bulle Shah</a>  is considered one of the greatest <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> poets. Punjabi <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> poetry developed under <a href="/wiki/Shah_Hussain" class="mw-redirect" title="Shah Hussain">Shah Hussain</a> (1538\xe2\x80\x931599), <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Bahu" title="Sultan Bahu">Sultan Bahu</a> (1628\xe2\x80\x931691), <a href="/wiki/Shah_Sharaf" title="Shah Sharaf">Shah Sharaf</a> (1640\xe2\x80\x931724), <a href="/wiki/Ali_Haider" title="Ali Haider">Ali Haider</a> (1690\xe2\x80\x931785), <a href="/wiki/Saleh_Muhammad_Safoori" title="Saleh Muhammad Safoori">Saleh Muhammad Safoori</a> (son of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hazrat_Mai_Safoora_Qadiriyya&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hazrat Mai Safoora Qadiriyya (page does not exist)">Hazrat Mai Safoora Qadiriyya</a>, whom Ali Haider had given great tribute) and <a href="/wiki/Bulleh_Shah" title="Bulleh Shah">Bulleh Shah</a> (1680\xe2\x80\x931757).</li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Punjab-Pak-Sufi_Poets.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Punjab-Pak-Sufi_Poets.jpg/220px-Punjab-Pak-Sufi_Poets.jpg" width="220" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Punjab-Pak-Sufi_Poets.jpg/330px-Punjab-Pak-Sufi_Poets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Punjab-Pak-Sufi_Poets.jpg/440px-Punjab-Pak-Sufi_Poets.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2470" data-file-height="2800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Punjab-Pak-Sufi_Poets.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sufi poets have enriched Punjabi literature</div></div></div>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikh</a> religion originated in the 15th century in the Punjab region and Punjabi is the predominant language spoken by Sikhs.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Most portions of the <a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a> use the Punjabi language written in <a href="/wiki/Gurmukh%C4%AB_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurmukh\xc4\xab alphabet">Gurmukhi</a>, though Punjabi is not the only language used in <a href="/wiki/Sikh_scriptures" title="Sikh scriptures">Sikh scriptures</a>.</li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Varan_Gyan_Ratnavali.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Varan_Gyan_Ratnavali.jpg/220px-Varan_Gyan_Ratnavali.jpg" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Varan_Gyan_Ratnavali.jpg/330px-Varan_Gyan_Ratnavali.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Varan_Gyan_Ratnavali.jpg/440px-Varan_Gyan_Ratnavali.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Varan_Gyan_Ratnavali.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Varan_Bhai_Gurdas" title="Varan Bhai Gurdas">Varan Gyan Ratnavali</a> by 16th-century historian <a href="/wiki/Bhai_Gurdas" title="Bhai Gurdas">Bhai Gurdas</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Janamsakhis" title="Janamsakhis">Janamsakhis</a></i> (\xe0\xa8\x9c\xe0\xa8\xa8\xe0\xa8\xae\xe0\xa8\xb8\xe0\xa8\xbe\xe0\xa8\x96\xe0\xa9\x80, <span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, IranNastaliq, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 125%; font-style: normal;">\xd8\xac\xd9\x86\xd9\x85 \xd8\xb3\xd8\xa7\xda\xa9\xda\xbe\xdb\x8c</span>&#x202c;), stories on the life and legend of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> (1469\xe2\x80\x931539), are early examples of Punjabi prose literature.\n</p>\n<ul><li>The Punjabi language is famous for its rich literature of <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_Qisse" title="Punjabi Qisse"><b>qisse</b></a> (\xe0\xa8\x95\xe0\xa8\xbf\xe0\xa9\xb1\xe0\xa8\xb8\xe0\xa9\x87, <span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, IranNastaliq, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 125%; font-style: normal;">\xd9\x82\xd8\xb5\xd9\x91\xdb\x92</span>&#x202c;), most of the which are about love, passion, betrayal, sacrifice, social values and a common man\'s revolt against a larger system. The qissa of <a href="/wiki/Heer_Ranjha" title="Heer Ranjha">Heer Ranjha</a> by <a href="/wiki/Waris_Shah" title="Waris Shah">Waris Shah</a> (1706\xe2\x80\x931798) is among the most popular of Punjabi qissas. Other popular stories include <i><a href="/wiki/Sohni_Mahiwal" title="Sohni Mahiwal">Sohni Mahiwal</a></i> by Fazal Shah, <i><a href="/wiki/Mirza_Sahiban" title="Mirza Sahiban">Mirza Sahiban</a></i> by Hafiz Barkhudar (1658\xe2\x80\x931707), <i><a href="/wiki/Sassui_Punnhun" title="Sassui Punnhun">Sassui Punnhun</a></i> by Hashim Shah (c. 1735\xe2\x80\x93c. 1843), and <i>Qissa Puran Bhagat</i> by <a href="/wiki/Qadaryar" class="mw-redirect" title="Qadaryar">Qadaryar</a> (1802\xe2\x80\x931892).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>\n<li>Heroic ballads known as <i><a href="/wiki/Vaar" title="Vaar">Vaar</a></i>(\xe0\xa8\xb5\xe0\xa8\xbe\xe0\xa8\xb0, <span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, IranNastaliq, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 125%; font-style: normal;">\xd9\x88\xd8\xa7\xd8\xb1</span>&#x202c;) enjoy a rich oral tradition in Punjabi. Famous <i>Vaars</i> are<i>Chandi di Var</i> (1666\xe2\x80\x931708), <i><a href="/wiki/Nadir_Shah" class="mw-redirect" title="Nadir Shah">Nadir Shah</a> Di Vaar</i> by Najabat,<i>Jangnama</i> of <a href="/wiki/Shah_Mohammad" title="Shah Mohammad">Shah Mohammad</a> (1780\xe2\x80\x931862).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Raj_era_and_post-independence_period">British Raj era and post-independence period</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ghadar_di_gunj.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ghadar_di_gunj.jpg/150px-Ghadar_di_gunj.jpg" width="150" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ghadar_di_gunj.jpg/225px-Ghadar_di_gunj.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ghadar_di_gunj.jpg/300px-Ghadar_di_gunj.jpg 2x" data-file-width="341" data-file-height="539" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ghadar_di_gunj.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Ghadar_di_Gunj" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghadar di Gunj">Ghadar di Gunj</a></i> 1913, newspaper in Punjabi of <a href="/wiki/Ghadar_Party" title="Ghadar Party">Ghadar Party</a>, US-based Indian revolutionary party.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Victorian novel, Elizabethan drama, free verse and <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">Modernism</a> entered Punjabi literature through the introduction of British education during the Raj. <a href="/wiki/Nanak_Singh" title="Nanak Singh">Nanak Singh</a> (1897\xe2\x80\x931971), Vir Singh, Ishwar Nanda, <a href="/wiki/Amrita_Pritam" title="Amrita Pritam">Amrita Pritam</a> (1919\xe2\x80\x932005), <a href="/wiki/Puran_Singh" title="Puran Singh">Puran Singh</a> (1881\xe2\x80\x931931), <a href="/wiki/Dhani_Ram_Chatrik" title="Dhani Ram Chatrik">Dhani Ram Chatrik</a> (1876\xe2\x80\x931957), <a href="/wiki/Diwan_Singh" title="Diwan Singh">Diwan Singh</a> (1897\xe2\x80\x931944) and <a href="/wiki/Ustad_Daman" title="Ustad Daman">Ustad Daman</a> (1911\xe2\x80\x931984), <a href="/wiki/Mohan_Singh_(poet)" title="Mohan Singh (poet)">Mohan Singh</a> (1905\xe2\x80\x9378) and <a href="/wiki/Shareef_Kunjahi" title="Shareef Kunjahi">Shareef Kunjahi</a> are some legendary Punjabi writers of this period.\nAfter independence of Pakistan and India Najm Hossein Syed, Fakhar Zaman and Afzal Ahsan Randhawa, <a href="/wiki/Shafqat_Tanvir_Mirza" title="Shafqat Tanvir Mirza">Shafqat Tanvir Mirza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Salim" title="Ahmad Salim">Ahmad Salim</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Najm_Hosain_Syed" title="Najm Hosain Syed">Najm Hosain Syed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Munir_Niazi" title="Munir Niazi">Munir Niazi</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pir_Hadi_abdul_Mannan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pir Hadi abdul Mannan (page does not exist)">Pir Hadi abdul Mannan</a> enriched Punjabi literature in Pakistan, whereas <a href="/wiki/Amrita_Pritam" title="Amrita Pritam">Amrita Pritam</a> (1919\xe2\x80\x932005), <a href="/wiki/Jaswant_Singh_Rahi" title="Jaswant Singh Rahi">Jaswant Singh Rahi</a> (1930\xe2\x80\x931996), <a href="/wiki/Shiv_Kumar_Batalvi" title="Shiv Kumar Batalvi">Shiv Kumar Batalvi</a> (1936\xe2\x80\x931973), <a href="/wiki/Surjit_Patar" title="Surjit Patar">Surjit Patar</a> (1944\xe2\x80\x93) and <a href="/wiki/Pash" title="Pash">Pash</a> (1950\xe2\x80\x931988) are some of the more prominent poets and writers from India.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Pakistan">In Pakistan</span></h2>\n<p>When <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> was created in 1947, although Punjabi was the majority language in <a href="/wiki/West_Pakistan" title="West Pakistan">West Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a> the majority in <a href="/wiki/East_Pakistan" title="East Pakistan">East Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> as whole, <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> were chosen as the national languages. The selection of Urdu was due to its association with South Asian Muslim nationalism and because the leaders of the new nation wanted a unifying national language instead of promoting one ethnic group\'s language over another. Broadcasting in Punjabi language by <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Pakistan Broadcasting Corporation">Pakistan Broadcasting Corporation</a> decreased on TV and radio after 1947.  Article 251 of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Pakistan" title="Constitution of Pakistan">Constitution of Pakistan</a> declares that these two languages would be the only official languages at the national level, while provincial governments would be allowed to make provisions for the use of other languages.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> However, in the 1950s the constitution was amended to include the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali language</a>. Eventually, Punjabi was granted status as a provincial language in Punjab Province, while the <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi language</a> was given official status in 1972 after <a href="/wiki/1972_Language_violence_in_Sindh" title="1972 Language violence in Sindh">1972 Language violence in Sindh</a>.\n</p><p>Despite gaining official recognition at the provincial level, Punjabi is not a language of instruction for primary or secondary school students in Punjab Province (unlike Sindhi and Pashto in other provinces).<sup id="cite_ref-Zaidi_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zaidi-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Pupils in secondary schools can choose the language as an elective, while Punjabi instruction or study remains rare in higher education. One notable example is the teaching of Punjabi language and literature by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Punjab" title="University of the Punjab">University of the Punjab</a> in Lahore which began in 1970 with the establishment of its Punjabi Department.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the cultural sphere, there are many books, plays, and songs being written or produced in the Punjabi-language in Pakistan. Until the 1970s, there were a large number of Punjabi-language films being produced by the Lollywood film industry, however since then Urdu has become a much more dominant language in film production. Additionally, television channels in Punjab Province (centred on the Lahore area) are broadcast in Urdu. The preeminence of Urdu in both broadcasting and the <a href="/wiki/Lollywood" title="Lollywood">Lollywood</a> film industry is seen by critics as being detrimental to the health of the language.<sup id="cite_ref-Masood_2015_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masood_2015-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Warraich_Ali_2015_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warraich_Ali_2015-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_demands_in_Punjab_province">Language demands in Punjab province</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Punjabi_prachar_demand.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Punjabi_prachar_demand.jpg/220px-Punjabi_prachar_demand.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Punjabi_prachar_demand.jpg/330px-Punjabi_prachar_demand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Punjabi_prachar_demand.jpg/440px-Punjabi_prachar_demand.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Punjabi_prachar_demand.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A demonstration by Punjabis at Lahore, Pakistan, demanding to make Punjabi as official language of instruction in schools of the Punjab.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pukheroo_kids_magazine_in_shahmukhi_script_of_punjabi_language_from_pakistan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Pukheroo_kids_magazine_in_shahmukhi_script_of_punjabi_language_from_pakistan.jpg/220px-Pukheroo_kids_magazine_in_shahmukhi_script_of_punjabi_language_from_pakistan.jpg" width="220" height="339" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/a9/Pukheroo_kids_magazine_in_shahmukhi_script_of_punjabi_language_from_pakistan.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="254" data-file-height="391" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pukheroo_kids_magazine_in_shahmukhi_script_of_punjabi_language_from_pakistan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>in Pakistan a very small number of newspapers, magazines are published in Punjabi language. Front cover of a kid\'s Punjabi magazine from Pakistan, to make Punjabi popular among children</div></div></div>\n<p>The use of Urdu and English as the near exclusive languages of broadcasting, the public sector, and formal education have led some to fear that Punjabi in Pakistan is being relegated to a low-status language and that it is being denied an environment where it can flourish. Several prominent educational leaders, researchers, and social commentators have echoed the opinion that the intentional promotion of Urdu and the continued denial of any official sanction or recognition of the Punjabi language amounts to a process of "Urdu-isation" that is detrimental to the health of the Punjabi language<sup id="cite_ref-apnaorg_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apnaorg-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In August 2015, the Pakistan Academy of Letters, International Writer\xe2\x80\x99s Council (IWC) and World Punjabi Congress (WPC) organised the <i>Khawaja Farid Conference</i> and demanded that a Punjabi-language university should be established in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> and that Punjabi language should be declared as the medium of instruction at the primary level.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Nation_2011_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Nation_2011-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In September 2015, a case was filed in <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Pakistan" title="Supreme Court of Pakistan">Supreme Court of Pakistan</a> against <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Government of Punjab, Pakistan">Government of Punjab, Pakistan</a> as it did not take any step to implement the Punjabi language in the province.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Nation_2015_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Nation_2015-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Business_Recorder_2015_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Business_Recorder_2015-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, several thousand Punjabis gather in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> every year on <a href="/wiki/International_Mother_Language_Day" title="International Mother Language Day">International Mother Language Day</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hafiz_Muhammad_Saeed" title="Hafiz Muhammad Saeed">Hafiz Saeed</a>, chief of Jama\'at-ud-Da\'wah (JuD) has questioned Pakistan\'s decision to adopt Urdu as its national language in a country where majority of people speak Punjabi language, citing his interpretation of Islamic doctrine as encouraging education in the mother-tongue.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>  The list of thinktanks, political organisations, cultural projects, and individuals that demand authorities at the national and provincial level to promote the use of the language in the public and official spheres includes:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Cultural and research institutes: Punjabi Adabi Board, the Khoj Garh Research Centre, Punjabi Prachar, Institute for Peace and Secular Studies, Adbi Sangat, Khaaksaar Tehreek, Saanjh, Maan Boli Research Centre, Punjabi Sangat Pakistan, Punjabi Markaz, Sver International</li>\n<li>Trade unions and youth groups:  Punjabi Writers Forum, National Students Federation, Punjabi Union-Pakistan, Punjabi National Conference, National Youth Forum, Punjabi Writers Forum, National Students Federation, Punjabi Union, Pakistan, and the Punjabi National Conference.</li>\n<li>Notable activists include Tariq Jatala, Farhad Iqbal, Diep Saeeda, Khalil Ojla, Afzal Sahir, Jamil Ahmad Paul, Mazhar Tirmazi, Mushtaq Sufi, Biya Je, Tohid Ahmad Chattha and Bilal Shaker Kahaloon, Nazeer Kahut<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_India">In India</span></h2>\n<p>At the federal level, Punjabi has official status via the <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Schedule_to_the_Indian_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="Eighth Schedule to the Indian Constitution">Eighth Schedule to the Indian Constitution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Khokhlova_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khokhlova-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> At the state level, Punjabi is the sole official language of the state of Punjab, while it has secondary official status in the states of Haryana and Delhi.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both federal and state laws specify the use of Punjabi in the field of education. The state of Punjab uses the Three Language Formula, and Punjabi is required to be either the medium of instruction, or one of the three languages learnt in all schools in Punjab.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Punjabi is also a compulsory language in Haryana,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> and other states with a significant Punjabi speaking minority are required to offer Punjabi medium education.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (October 2017)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Punjabi_language#Dubious" title="Talk:Punjabi language">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>There is a vibrant Punjabi language movie industry in India, however Punjabi has a much smaller presence in television.<sup id="cite_ref-Singh_2015_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singh_2015-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Despite Punjabi having far greater official recognition in India, attitudes of the Punjabi speaking elite towards the language are ambivalent as they are in neighboring Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-Khokhlova_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khokhlova-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>37</span></sup> There are also claims of state apathy towards the language in non-Punjabi majority states.<sup id="cite_ref-http&#58;//www.hindustantimes.com/_2015_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-http://www.hindustantimes.com/_2015-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aujla_2015_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aujla_2015-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Singh_2013_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singh_2013-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutes_working_for_Punjabi">Institutes working for Punjabi</span></h2>\n<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Punjabi_University" title="Punjabi University">Punjabi University</a></b>  It was established on the 30 April 1962, and is only the second university in the world to be named after a language, after <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_University_of_Jerusalem" title="Hebrew University of Jerusalem">Hebrew University of Jerusalem</a>.</li></ul>\n<ol><li>Research Centre for Punjabi Language Technology, Punjabi University, Patiala.<sup id="cite_ref-punjabiuniversity_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-punjabiuniversity-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> It is working for development of core technologies for Punjabi, Digitisation of basic materials, online Punjabi teaching, developing software for office use in Punjabi, provinding common platform to Punjabi cyber community.<sup id="cite_ref-learnpunjabi2_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-learnpunjabi2-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <b>Machine translation tool</b> for Punjabi to Hindi, Punjabi to Urdu nad vice versa and <b>machine transliteration system</b> between Gurumukhi and Shahmukhi scripts are very popular.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Punjabipedia" title="Punjabipedia">Punjabipedia</a> an online encyclopaedia is also launched by Patiala university in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-punjabipedia_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-punjabipedia-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hindustantimes_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hindustantimes-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>\n<ul><li><b>The Dhahan Prize</b>: <a href="/wiki/The_Dhahan_Prize" title="The Dhahan Prize">The Dhahan Prize</a> was created award literary works produced in Punjabi around the world. The Prize encourages new writing by awarding $25,000 CDN annually to one "best book of fiction" published in either of the two Punjabi scripts, <b>Gurmukhi</b> or <b>Shahmukhi</b>. Two second prizes of $5,000 CDN are also awarded, with the provision that both scripts are represented among the three winners. The Dhahan Prize is awarded by Canada India Education Society (CIES).<sup id="cite_ref-dhahanprize_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhahanprize-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governmental_academies_and_institutes">Governmental academies and institutes</span></h2>\n<table PILAC also focuses on conservation, protection, promotion and enhancement of the art and cultural richness of Punjab.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Software">Software</span></h2>\n<ul><li>Software are available for Punjabi language for almost all platforms. These software are mainly in <a href="/wiki/Gurmukhi" title="Gurmukhi">Gurmukhi</a> script. Nowadays, nearly all Punjabi newspapers, magazines, journals, and periodicals are composed on computers via various Punjabi software programmes, the most widespread of which is <a href="/wiki/InPage" title="InPage">InPage</a> Desktop Publishing package. <b><a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a></b> has included Punjabi language support in all new versions of Windows and both <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">Windows Vista</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_2007" title="Microsoft Office 2007">Mircrsoft Office 2007</a>, 2010 and 2013, are available in Punjabi through the <a href="/wiki/Language_Interface_Pack" title="Language Interface Pack">Language Interface Pack</a><sup id="cite_ref-microsoft_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-microsoft-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> support. Most <b><a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a></b> Desktop distributions allow the easy installation of Punjabi support and translations as well.<sup id="cite_ref-sourceforge_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sourceforge-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> <b><a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a></b> implemented the Punjabi language keyboard across <a href="/wiki/Mobile_device" title="Mobile device">Mobile devices</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-apple_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apple-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> <b><a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a></b> also provides many applications in Punjabi, like Google Search,<sup id="cite_ref-google6_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google6-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Google Translate<sup id="cite_ref-google7_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google7-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> and Google Punjabi Input Tools.<sup id="cite_ref-google8_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google8-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery">Gallery</span></h2>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n\t\t<li
